Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Impossibility of Two Simultaneous Opinions for a Mujtahid
Explains the Hanafi usul position on imkan al-qawlayn with taharri and temporal succession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul rejects the possibility of a mujtahid having two simultaneous opinions and apply the taharri method to resolve conflicting evidences.

Core Idea
A mujtahid cannot hold two contradictory opinions at once; two narrations reflect temporal succession, not simultaneous validity.

This lesson examines a foundational principle in Hanafi usul: the impossibility of a mujtahid holding two contradictory opinions simultaneously (imkan al-qawlayn). The passage contrasts the Hanafi method of taharri (seeking the strongest evidence) with the Shafi'i approach that permits choice between conflicting proofs. You will see how the Hanafi school reconciles multiple narrations from a single Imam and why the practical consequence is that followers must seek the later opinion when known.

Guided Path
1
State the core dispute
Identify the Hanafi position (no simultaneous opinions) versus the Shafi'i position (possible). Note the key mechanism: taharri vs. free choice.
The Hanafi position is built on the principle that a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory intentions at the same time.
2
Understand the taharri process
Follow the hierarchy of conflicting evidence: Quran → Hadith → Companion statements → qiyas. If two qiyas conflict and no tarjih, the mujtahid performs taharri and acts on what his heart leans to. After acting, he cannot switch without stronger evidence.
Taharri is a method for the mujtahid personally, not for the muqallid.
3
Reconcile two narrations from the same Imam
Two narrations from a Hanafi imam (e.g., Abu Hanifa) must come from different times. One is later and supersedes the other, but the later one may be unknown. Hence, scholars say 'there are two narrations' due to ignorance of the chronological order.
This avoids the logical problem of simultaneous contradictory opinions.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For a Hanafi follower: when two narrations exist and the later is known, it must be followed. When unknown, mujtahidun may investigate but cannot freely choose. For Shafi'is: the follower may choose either opinion.
This difference impacts fatwa and taqlid.
5
Critique and resolution
The passage notes a problem: if both narrations are attributed to the Imam but we don't know which is later, acting on either seems problematic. Yet scholars still weigh and attribute one to him. The author leans toward the explanation of Imam al-Balighi that multiplicity arises from different contexts.
This shows the living nature of ijtihad within the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إمكان القولين لمجتهد
لكن ما ذكر في كتب الأصول عندنا من أنه لا يمكن أن يكون للمجتهد قولان كما مر - ينافي ذلك؛ لأنه مبني – فيما يظهر – على ما ذكروا في تعارض الأدلة أنه إذا وقع التعارض بين آيتين يصار إلى الحديث، فإن تعارض فإلى أقوال الصحابة فإن تعارضت فإلى القياس، فإن تعارض قياسان ولا ترجيح فإنه يتحرى فيهما، ويعمل بشهادة قلبه، فإذا عمل بأحدهما، ليس له العمل بالآخر إلا بدليل فوق التحري. قالوا: وقال الشافعي يعمل بأيهما شاء من غير تحر. ولهذا صار له في المسألة قولان وأكثر. وأما الروايتان عن أصحابنا في مسألة واحدة، فإنما كانتا في وقتين، فإحداهما صحيحة دون الأخرى، لكن لم تعرف المتأخرة منهما.
وعلى هذا فما يقال: "فيه عن الإمام روايتان، فلعدم معرفة الأخير، وما يقال فيه: "وفي رواية عنه كذا إما لعلمهم بأنها قوله الأول، أو لكون هذه الرواية رويت عنه في غير كتب الأصول، وهذا أقرب. لكن لا يخفى أن ما ذكروه في بحث تعارض الأدلة مشكل؛ لأنه يلزم منه أن يكون ما فيه روايتان عن الإمام لا يجوز فيه العمل بواحدة منهما؛ لعدم العلم بالصحيحة من الباطلة منهما، وأنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما، كما مر عن بعض الأصوليين، عن بعض الأصوليين، مع أن ذلك واقع في مسائل لا تحصى، ونراهم يرجحون إحدى الروايتين على الأخرى، وينسبونها إليه. فالذي يظهر ما مر عن الإمام البليغي من بيان تعدد
Possibility of two opinions for a mujtahid. However, what is mentioned in the usul books with us—that a mujtahid cannot have two opinions as previously stated—contradicts that. Because it is based, as it appears, on what they mentioned regarding conflicting evidence: that when conflict occurs between two Quranic verses, one turns to hadith; if they conflict, to the statements of the Companions; if they conflict, to qiyas; if two qiyas conflict and there is no preponderance, then one exercises taharri (seeking the strongest) and acts according to the testimony of his heart. Once he acts by one, he may not act by the other without evidence above taharri. They said: And al-Shafi'i said he acts by whichever he wishes without taharri. Hence he can have two or more opinions in an issue. As for the two narrations from our scholars in a single issue, they were from two different times, so one is correct and the other not, but the later one is not known. On this basis, what is said: 'There are two narrations from the Imam' is due to not knowing the later one, and what is said: 'In a narration from him such-and-such' is either because they know it is his earlier opinion, or because this narration was narrated from him in non-usul works, and this is more plausible. But it is not hidden that what they mentioned in the discussion of conflicting evidence is problematic; because it implies that what has two narrations from the Imam cannot be acted upon by either due to not knowing which is correct, and that nothing is attributed to him, as mentioned from some usul scholars. Yet this occurs in countless issues, and we see them preferring one narration over another and attributing it to him. So what appears is what was mentioned from Imam al-Balighi regarding the explanation of multiplicity...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi principle that a mujtahid cannot have two simultaneous opinions (imkan al-qawlayn). It contrasts the Hanafi method of taharri (seeking the strongest) with the Shafi'i method of choosing freely. In Hanafi usul, when evidence conflicts, one must perform taharri to determine the stronger, and cannot switch arbitrarily. For Shafi'i, the mujtahid may choose either. Hence, two simultaneous opinions are possible for al-Shafi'i but not for Hanafis. The passage explains that two narrations from a Hanafi imam are from different times; one is later and abrogates the other, but the later may be unknown. This is why scholars sometimes say 'there are two narrations'—due to ignorance of the later one. The passage also critiques the logical consequence: if both narrations are valid but one is unknown, then neither can be acted upon, yet in practice, scholars weigh and attribute one to the Imam. The author leans toward the explanation of Imam al-Balighi (al-Bulghī?) that there is multiplicity due to different contexts.

Key Terms
تحري (taharri): The process of seeking the strongest evidence when conflicting proofs are balanced
رواية (riwayah): A transmitted version of a mujtahid's opinion, often from different time periods
إمكان القولين (imkan al-qawlayn): The possibility of a mujtahid having two opinions on the same issue at the same time

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason why two narrations from a Hanafi imam on a single issue do not constitute two simultaneous opinions?
A) The narrations are from different imams.
B) The narrations are from different times, one being the later correct opinion.
C) The imam allowed both but the madhhab chose one.
D) The narrations are both weak and not reliable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, two opinions from a mujtahid cannot be simultaneous; multiple narrations reflect temporal succession, and the later ruling must be followed when known.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Rule of Following the Founding Imams and Exception for Necessity
Discussion on when a mufti may depart from the consensus of Hanafi founders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule regarding following the founding imams and apply the exception for necessity.

Core Idea
A mufti must follow agreed-upon rulings of founding imams, but necessity permits departure.

This passage addresses a critical tension in Hanafi iftaa: when may a mufti depart from the consensus of the founding imams? While the default rule is strict adherence to the agreed-upon rulings of Zahir al-Riwayah, the author introduces a crucial exception: necessity. The example of hiring for Quran teaching illustrates how preserving religious practice can override the default prohibition. Understanding this rule and its exception is essential for muftis who must balance fidelity to the school with practical needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
From Qadi Khan's Rasm al-Mufti: if a ruling is narrated without disagreement in the apparent narrations (Zahir al-Riwayah), the mufti must follow it and not oppose it with his own opinion, even if he is a qualified mujtahid.
This reflects the hierarchy where the imams' ijtihad is considered stronger.
2
Recognize the restriction from Ibn al-Humam
Ibn al-Humam states that one should not deviate from Imam Abu Hanifa's statement unless his evidence is weak. The author qualifies this restriction: it applies only when Abu Hanifa's view agrees with a position in the madhhab.
This prevents ijtihad that completely abandons the school's established consensus.
3
Locate the exception for necessity
The author asserts that the imams themselves may have deviated from their own rulings due to necessity, as in the case of hiring for Quran teaching—initially prohibited, later permitted because leaving it would cause loss to the religion.
Necessity (darurah) is a recognized grounds for departure from the default rule.
4
Connect to the broader principle
The maxim 'al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat' (necessity permits the prohibited) underpins this exception. The mufti must assess whether adhering to the strict rule would lead to harm to the religion.
This is not a blanket permission; it requires genuine necessity.
5
Determine the operative fatwa
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) allows departure for necessity, as affirmed by the author and references to al-Hawi al-Qudsi. The mufti must be careful to apply this only when the condition of necessity is met.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Ibn al-Humam's stricter view is not followed; necessity is a valid exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العمل بالحديث إذا صح
ابن الهمام على المشايخ حيث أفتوا بقول الإمامين، بأنه لا يعدل عن قول الإمام إلا لضعف دليله.
وأقول أيضا ينبغي تقييد ذلك بما إذا وافق قولا في المذهب؛ إذ لم يأذنوا في الاجتهاد فيما خرج عن المذهب بالكلية مما اتفق عليه أئمتنا؛ لأن اجتهادهم أقوى من اجتهاده فالظاهر أنهم رأوا دليلا أرجح مما رآه حتى لم يعملوا به، ولهذا قال العلامة قاسم في حق شيخه خاتمة المحققين الكمال بن الهمام: لا يعمل بأبحاث شيخنا التي تخالف المذهب، وقال في تصحيحه على القدوري: قال الإمام العلامة الحسن بن منصور بن محمود الأوزجندي المعروف بـ"قاضي خان" في كتاب الفتاوى: "رسم المفتي في زماننا من أصحابنا إذا استفتي عن مسألة إن كانت مروية عن أصحابنا في الروايات الظاهرة بلا خلاف بينهم، فإنه يميل إليهم ويفتي بقولهم، ولا يخالفهم برأيه وإن كان مجتهدا متقنا؛ لأن الظاهر أن يكون الحق مع أصحابنا ولا يعدوهم واجتهاده لا يبلغ اجتهادهم، ولا ينظر إلى قول من خالفهم، ولا تقبل حجته أيضا؛ لأنهم عرفوا الأدلة، وميزوا بين ما صح وثبت وبين ضده إلخ. ثم نقل نحوه عن شرح برهان الأئمة على أدب القضاء للخصاف.
:قلت لكن ربما عدلوا عما اتفق عليه أئمتنا لضرورة ونحوها، كما مر في مسألة الاستئجار على تعليم القرآن ونحوه من الطاعات التي في ترك الاستئجار عليها ضياع الدين كما قررناه سابقا، فحينئذ يجوز الإفتاء بخلاف قولهم، كما نذكره قريبا عن "الحاوي القدسي"، وسيأتي بسطه أيضا آخر الشرح عند الكلام على العرف.
Action based on the hadith if it is authentic. Ibn al-Humam (criticized) the scholars who gave fatwa according to the statement of the two Imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), saying that one should not deviate from the statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) except due to weakness of his evidence. And I say: This should also be restricted to when (the Imam's statement) agrees with a saying in the madhhab; for they did not permit ijtihad in matters that are completely outside the madhhab upon which our Imams are agreed; because their ijtihad is stronger than his ijtihad, and apparently they saw a stronger evidence than what he saw such that they did not act upon it. That is why Allama Qasim said concerning his teacher, the seal of the verifiers Kamal ibn al-Humam: 'One should not act upon the researches of our teacher that contradict the madhhab.' And he said in his correction of al-Quduri: Imam al-Allama al-Hasan ibn Mansur ibn Mahmud al-Uzjandi, known as Qadi Khan, said in his book al-Fatawa: 'The rule of the mufti in our time from our companions: if he is asked about a matter that is narrated from our companions in the apparent narrations without any disagreement among them, then he inclines to them and gives fatwa according to their statement, and he does not oppose them with his opinion, even if he is a qualified mujtahid; because the apparent is that the truth is with our companions and does not go beyond them, and his ijtihad does not reach their ijtihad. He does not look at the statement of those who oppose them, nor is his evidence accepted; because they knew the evidences and distinguished between what is sound and established and its opposite, etc.' Then he transmitted similar from Sharh Burhan al-A'imma 'ala Adab al-Qada by al-Khassaf. I said: But perhaps they deviated from what our Imams agreed upon due to necessity and the like, as has passed in the issue of hiring for teaching the Quran and similar acts of obedience, in which leaving hiring causes loss to the religion, as we have established earlier. So in that case, it is permissible to give fatwa contrary to their statement, as we will mention soon from al-Hawi al-Qudsi, and its expansion will also come later in the commentary at the discussion of custom.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a rasm rule (methodological rule for muftis) concerning the authority of the founding Hanafi imams. The author begins by referencing Ibn al-Humam's position that a mufti should not depart from the ruling of Abu Hanifa (the Imam) unless the evidence for that ruling is weak. Ibn al-Humam criticized scholars who preferred the opinions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) over Abu Hanifa without such weakness. The author then qualifies this: even Ibn al-Humam's rule must be restricted to cases where Abu Hanifa's opinion aligns with an existing position within the madhhab. The scholars did not permit ijtihad that completely abandons the established consensus of the imams, because their collective ijtihad is stronger. The author then quotes Qadi Khan's 'Rasm al-Mufti' passage, which states that the mufti must follow the apparent narrations (Zahir al-Riwayah) that are uncontested, and not oppose them with his own opinion. Finally, the author adds an important exception: necessity. Citing the example of hiring for teaching the Quran (which was initially prohibited but later permitted due to necessity to preserve religious education), he notes that if adhering to the agreed-upon ruling would lead to harm to the religion, it is permissible to give fatwa contrary to it. This shows that the rasm rule has flexibility for urgent needs.

Key Terms
رسم المفتي (rasm al-mufti): The methodological rule guiding a mufti on which opinions to prefer and how to issue fatwas within the Hanafi school.
الرواية الظاهرة (al-riwaya al-zahira): The apparent and openly transmitted narrations of the madhhab, i.e., the six books of Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what circumstance may a mufti depart from the consensus of the founding Hanafi imams?
A) Whenever the mufti's personal ijtihad leads to a different conclusion.
B) Only if the evidence for the Imam's ruling is weak.
C) When necessity arises, such as to prevent loss to the religion.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rasm al-mufti requires following the consensus of the founding imams, but necessity (like preserving religious practice) permits departure from their agreed-upon ruling.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Takhrījāt and Attribution in the Hanafi Madhhab
Rasm al-mufti rule on the status and attribution of takhrījāt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify takhrījāt (derived rulings) and apply the correct attribution method (muqtada al-madhhab) when describing rulings derived from Imam Abu Hanifa’s principles.

Core Idea
Derived rulings are part of the madhhab but must be attributed as 'muqtada al-madhhab', not directly to Abu Hanifa.

In the Hanafi school, not every ruling attributed to the madhhab was explicitly stated by Imam Abu Hanifa. Later scholars derived rulings (takhrījāt) from his principles to address new cases. This lesson clarifies the status of these derivations and the precise language muftis must use when attributing them, ensuring scholarly integrity while maintaining the living nature of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of rulings in the madhhab
Distinguish between explicit statements of Abu Hanifa (manqūl) and rulings derived from his principles (takhrījāt). The latter are not directly attributed to him.
Explicit statements are rare; most rulings are from disciples or derived.
2
Understand the conditions for takhrījāt to be part of the madhhab
Derived rulings remain within the madhhab if they are based on the Imam's qawāʿid, even if they differ from his explicit statement, provided they are preponderated by authoritative scholars.
Change of custom or necessity also justifies derivation.
3
Apply the correct attribution rule
For takhrījāt, never say 'Abū Ḥanīfa said X'. Instead, say 'muqtaḍā al-madhhab of Abū Ḥanīfa is X' or 'on the analogy of his statement X'.
This preserves the integrity of the Imam's explicit statements.
4
Examine the application in judicial rulings
A judge who rules according to a derived opinion (e.g., of Abū Yūsuf or Muḥammad) is not ruling contrary to the madhhab, because such opinions are still within the school.
The passage cites al-Durar wa al-Ghrar to illustrate this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تخريجات المشايخ على قواعد الإمام
والحاصل أن ما خالف فيها الأصحاب إمامهم الأعظم، لا يخرج عن مذهبه إذا رجحه المشايخ المعتبرون، وكذا ما بناه المشايخ على العرف الحادث لتغير الزمان أو للضرورة، ونحو ذلك لا يخرج عن مذهبه أيضاً؛ لأن ما رجحوه لترجح دليله عندهم مأذون به من جهة الإمام، وكذا ما بنوه على تغير الزمان والضرورة، باعتبار أنه لو كان حيا لقال بما قالوه؛
لأن ما قالوه إنما هو مبني على قواعده أيضا، فهو مقتضى مذهبه. لكن ينبغي أن لا يقال: "قال أبو حنيفة كذا"، إلا فيما روي عنه صريحا، وإنما يقال فيه: "مقتضى مذهب أبي حنيفة كذا"، كما قلنا. ومثله تخريجات المشايخ بعض الأحكام من قواعده، أو بالقياس على قوله، ومنه قولهم: "وعلى قياس قوله بكذا يكون كذا"، فهذا كله لا يقال فيه: "قال أبو حنيفة"، نعم! يصح أن يسمى مذهبه بمعنى أنه قول أهل مذهبه، أو مقتضى مذهبه.
وعن هذا لمّا قال صاحب "الدرر والغرر" في كتاب القضاء: "إذا قضى القاضي في مجتهد فيه بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ" - قال: "أي أصل المذهب كالحنفي إذا حكم على مذهب الشافعي أو نحوه، أو بالعكس، وأما إذا حكم الحنفي بمذهب أبي يوسف، أو محمد، أو نحوهما من أصحاب الإمام، فليس حكما بخلاف رأيه". والظاهر أن نسبة المسائل المخرّجة إلى مذهبه أقرب من نسبة المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف أو محمد إليه؛ لأن المخرجة مبنية على قواعده وأصوله، وأما المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف ونحوه من أصحاب
Derivations of the scholars on the principles of the Imam. The summary is that whatever the companions (the Hanafi scholars) differed from their greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) does not leave his madhhab if the authoritative scholars have preponderated it. Similarly, what the scholars built upon a newly arisen custom due to change of time or necessity, and the like, does not leave his madhhab either; because what they preponderated due to its evidence being stronger in their view is permitted from the Imam's side. Likewise, what they built upon change of time and necessity, considering that if he (the Imam) were alive, he would have said what they said; because what they said is based on his principles as well, so it is the requirement of his madhhab. But one should not say: 'Abu Hanifa said such and such' except concerning what is explicitly narrated from him. Rather, one should say: 'The requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such and such,' as we have said. The same applies to the derivations of the scholars of some rulings from his principles, or by analogy on his statement, including their saying: 'And on the analogy of his statement such and such, it is such and such.' All of this should not be attributed as 'Abu Hanifa said.' Yes, it is correct to call it his madhhab in the sense that it is the statement of the people of his madhhab, or the requirement of his madhhab. Concerning this, when the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghrar said in the book of Judgments: 'If a judge judges in a matter of ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, his judgment is not enforceable' — he said: 'That is, the original madhhab, e.g., a Hanafi judges according to the Shafi'i madhhab or the like, or vice versa. But if a Hanafi judges according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad, or similar from the companions of the Imam, it is not a judgment contrary to his opinion.' And apparently, attributing derived (mukharraja) cases to his madhhab is closer than attributing cases that Abu Yusuf or Muhammad stated to him; because the derived cases are built on his principles and foundations, whereas cases stated by Abu Yusuf and similar companions...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage provides a crucial rasm rule regarding the status of takhrījāt (derived rulings) within the Hanafi madhhab. The author summarizes that when later scholars (the ashab) differ from Abu Hanifa and prefer a different ruling based on stronger evidence, that ruling does not leave the madhhab. Similarly, rulings built on new customs or necessity are also within the madhhab, because they are derived from the Imam's own principles, assuming he would have agreed if he were alive. However, the author imposes a critical terminological distinction: one must not say 'Abu Hanifa said X' for such derived rulings; rather, say 'the requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa (muqtada al-madhhab) is X.' This applies to direct derivations and analogies. The author then links this to a statement from al-Durar wa al-Ghrar: a judge's ruling contrary to his madhhab is invalid if it contradicts the original madhhab (e.g., Hanafi vs Shafi'i), but not if it follows the opinion of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad, as those are still within the madhhab. The passage concludes by noting that derived cases are even closer to the Imam's madhhab than the independent opinions of his disciples, because they are based on his usul.

Key Terms
تخريجات المشايخ (takhrījāt al-mashāyikh): Rulings derived by later Hanafi scholars from the principles and qiyas of Imam Abu Hanifa, not explicitly stated by him.
مقتضى المذهب (muqtadā al-madhhab): The logical requirement or implication of the madhhab derived from its principles, used to describe rulings not explicitly stated by the founder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How should a ruling derived from Imam Abu Hanifa's principles be correctly attributed according to the passage?
A) As 'Abu Hanifa said such and such.'
B) As 'The requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such and such.'
C) As 'The opinion of Abu Yusuf.'
D) As 'A ruling outside the madhhab.'

Key Takeaway
Takhrījāt are part of the Hanafi madhhab but must be attributed as 'muqtada al-madhhab', never as direct statements of Abu Hanifa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Takhrijat and Attributing Rulings to the Hanafi Madhhab
Explaining the status of takhrijat and rules of attribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a ruling can be attributed to the Hanafi madhhab and when it must be expressed as 'muqtada al-madhhab' rather than a direct statement of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Takhrijat built on the Imam's principles are part of the madhhab but must not be attributed to Abu Hanifa by name.

A mufti must know the hierarchy of sources within the madhhab. This passage clarifies the difference between explicit reports from Abu Hanifa and the juristic deductions (takhrijat) of later scholars based on his principles. While takhrijat are valid and enforceable, they require careful phrasing to avoid misattribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of rulings discussed
The passage covers three categories: explicit statements from Abu Hanifa, disagreements of the Sahibayn later preferred by mashayikh, and takhrijat (deductions) by later scholars using qiyas or istihsan.
Focus on the distinction between riwayah (transmission) and derayah (deduction).
2
State the rule of attribution
The rule is clear: only explicitly reported statements from Abu Hanifa may be prefaced with 'قال أبو حنيفة'. All other rulings, even if derived from his principles, must be attributed as 'مقتضى مذهب أبي حنيفة' (the requirement of his madhhab).
This preserves the integrity of the Imam's transmitted words.
3
Understand the justification for takhrijat
Takhrijat are considered part of the madhhab because they are built solely on Abu Hanifa's usul and qawaid. The author argues that if the Imam were alive, he would have issued the same ruling, because the deduction follows his methodology.
The phrase 'باعتبار أنه لو كان حيا لقال بما قالوه' captures this reasoning.
4
Compare takhrijat with Sahibayn opinions
The passage asserts that takhrijat are even closer to the Imam's madhhab than the independent opinions of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad al-Shaybani when those opinions are based on principles that opposed Abu Hanifa's own usul.
However, if the Sahibayn's statements are riwayat from the Imam, they are also valid; but takhrijat are more exclusively based on his principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن ما خالف فيها الأصحاب إمامهم الأعظم، لا يخرج عن مذهبه إذا رجحه المشايخ المعتبرون، وكذا ما بناه المشايخ على العرف الحادث لتغير الزمان أو للضرورة، ونحو ذلك لا يخرج عن مذهبه أيضاً؛ لأن ما رجحوه لترجح دليله عندهم مأذون به من جهة الإمام، وكذا ما بنوه على تغير الزمان والضرورة، باعتبار أنه لو كان حيا لقال بما قالوه؛ لأن ما قالوه إنما هو مبني على قواعده أيضا، فهو مقتضى مذهبه. لكن ينبغي أن لا يقال: "قال أبو حنيفة كذا"، إلا فيما روي عنه صريحا، وإنما يقال فيه: "مقتضى مذهب أبي حنيفة كذا"، كما قلنا. ومثله تخريجات المشايخ بعض الأحكام من قواعده، أو بالقياس على قوله، ومنه قولهم: "وعلى قياس قوله بكذا يكون كذا"، فهذا كله لا يقال فيه: "قال أبو حنيفة"، نعم! يصح أن يسمى مذهبه بمعنى أنه قول أهل مذهبه، أو مقتضى مذهبه. وعن هذا لمّا قال صاحب "الدرر والغرر" في كتاب القضاء: "إذا قضى القاضي في مجتهد فيه بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ" - قال: "أي أصل المذهب كالحنفي إذا حكم على مذهب الشافعي أو نحوه، أو بالعكس، وأما إذا حكم الحنفي بمذهب أبي يوسف، أو محمد، أو نحوهما من أصحاب الإمام، فليس حكما بخلاف رأيه". والظاهر أن نسبة المسائل المخرّجة إلى مذهبه أقرب من نسبة المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف أو محمد إليه؛ لأن المخرجة مبنية على قواعده وأصوله، وأما المسائل التي قال بها أبو يوسف ونحوه من أصحاب الإمام، فكثير منها مبني على قواعد لهم خالفوا فيها قواعد الإمام؛ لأنهم لم يلتزموا قواعده كلها، كما يعرفه من له معرفة بكتب الأصول. نعم! قد يقال: إذا كانت أقوالهم روايات عنه - على ما مر – تكون تلك القواعد له أيضا لابتناء تلك الأقوال عليها. وعلى هذا أيضا تكون نسبة التخريجات إلى مذهبه أقرب؛ لابتنائها على قواعده التي رجحها وبنى أقواله عليها. فإذا قضى القاضي بما صح منها، نفذ قضاؤه، كما ينفذ بما صح من أقوال الأصحاب. فهذا ما ظهر لي تقريره في هذا الباب من فتح الملك الوهاب، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب، وإليه المرجع والمآب.
The conclusion is that what the companions disagreed with their greatest Imam regarding does not leave his madhhab if the reliable scholars give it preference. Likewise, what the scholars built upon emerging custom due to change of time or necessity, and the like, also does not leave his madhhab; because what they preferred due to its evidence being stronger in their view is authorized by the Imam, and likewise what they built upon change of time and necessity, on the basis that if he were alive he would have said what they said; because what they said is also based on his principles, so it is the requirement of his madhhab. However, one should not say 'Abu Hanifa said such and such' except in what is explicitly reported from him. Rather, one should say about it: 'The requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such and such,' as we have said. Similarly, the juristic deductions of the scholars of some rulings from his principles, or by analogy to his statement, including their saying 'And by analogy to his statement about such and such, it is such and such' - all of this should not be described as 'Abu Hanifa said.' Yes! It is valid to call it his madhhab in the sense that it is the statement of the people of his madhhab, or the requirement of his madhhab. Regarding this, when the author of 'al-Durar wa al-Ghurar' said in the Book of Judgments: 'If a judge passes judgment in a matter open to ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, it is not enforceable' - he said: 'Meaning the original madhhab, such as a Hanafi if he judges according to the Shafi'i madhhab or similar, or vice versa. As for when a Hanafi judges according to the opinion of Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad, or others among the companions of the Imam, it is not a judgment contrary to his opinion.' And it seems that attribution of the derived issues to his madhhab is closer than the attribution of issues said by Abu Yusuf or Muhammad to him; because the derived issues are built on his principles and foundations. As for the issues said by Abu Yusuf and others among the companions of the Imam, many of them are based on principles of theirs in which they opposed the principles of the Imam, because they did not adhere to all his principles, as known to anyone familiar with the books of usul. Yes! It may be said: If their statements are transmissions from him - as previously mentioned - then those principles belong to him as well due to those statements being based on them. According to this as well, the attribution of the derivations to his madhhab is closer, because they are based on his principles which he favored and upon which he built his statements. So if a judge judges according to what is sound from them, his judgment is enforceable, just as it is enforceable according to what is sound from the statements of the companions. This is what appeared to me to establish in this chapter from 'Fath al-Malik al-Wahhab'. And Allah knows best the truth, and to Him is the return and refuge.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule concerning the juristic deductions (takhrijat) of later Hanafi scholars and how they relate to the madhhab. It begins by stating the general principle: when the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa, if later reliable scholars (mashayikh) prefer one of their views, that view is still considered part of the Hanafi madhhab. Similarly, rulings built upon newly emerging customs or necessity are also considered part of the madhhab because they are based on the Imam's own principles. The reasoning is that the later scholars are authorized to give preference based on stronger evidence, and if the Imam were alive, he would likely agree with them. However, a key practical rule is that one must not say 'Abu Hanifa said' for any ruling that is not explicitly reported from him. Instead, one should use the phrase 'the requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such and such.' This includes takhrijat and analogical extensions. The author then cites from 'al-Durar wa al-Ghurar' to illustrate the point: a Hanafi judge who judges according to the opinion of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad is not judging contrary to his madhhab. Furthermore, the author argues that the derived rulings (takhrijat) are even closer to the Imam's madhhab than the direct statements of the Sahibayn when they differ from him. This is because takhrijat are built solely on the Imam's principles and usul, while the Sahibayn sometimes relied on their own principles that conflicted with his. However, if the Sahibayn's statements are considered transmissions (riwayat) from the Imam, then their principles also become his. Thus, takhrijat remain the most faithful to the Imam's methodology. The passage concludes that a judge's ruling based on sound takhrijat is enforceable.

Key Terms
تخريجات المشايخ (takhrijat al-mashayikh): Rulings derived by later Hanafi scholars from the principles and usul of Imam Abu Hanifa, often by qiyas or ijtihad.
مقتضى المذهب (muqtada al-madhhab): The logical requirement or implication of the madhhab's principles, used for rulings not explicitly reported from the Imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when should a ruling be attributed as 'قال أبو حنيفة كذا'?
A) When it is derived by qiyas from his principles
B) When it is explicitly reported from him
C) When the mashayikh prefer it
D) When it is based on custom

Key Takeaway
The rule of attribution: explicit reports from Abu Hanifa are directly attributed; all derivations based on his principles must be expressed as 'muqtada al-madhhab'.

Hierarchy of Opinions in the Hanafi Madhhab (Tartib al-Riwayat)
Explains the order of precedence when Hanafi imams disagree

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy of opinions to determine the mufta bihi (operative opinion) when the founding imams disagree.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi madhhab, the order of precedence for fatwa is: Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan.

This lesson explains the established order of precedence among the narrations and opinions within the Hanafi school. When the founding imams differ on a ruling, the mufti must follow a specific sequence to determine which opinion to give as fatwa. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for anyone issuing fatwas within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of disagreement
Recognize that the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Hasan) may have different opinions on a given issue.
This is the starting point: there must be a known ikhtilaf.
2
Check for Abu Hanifa's ikhtiyar
Determine whether Abu Hanifa has a chosen opinion (ikhtiyar) on the matter. If he does, that opinion takes precedence.
The ikhtiyar is his preferred view among multiple narrations from him.
3
Move to Abu Yusuf if no ikhtiyar from Abu Hanifa
If no ikhtiyar exists from Abu Hanifa, then the opinion chosen by Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub) is given precedence.
Abu Yusuf is the greatest of Abu Hanifa's disciples.
4
Proceed to Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar and al-Hasan
If neither Abu Hanifa nor Abu Yusuf has a clear ikhtiyar, then Muhammad's opinion is used. After that, the opinions of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad are considered; they are of equal rank.
Some sources specify al-Hasan ibn Ziyad after Zufar, but both are in the same tier.
5
Consider exceptions for a mujtahid mufti
If the mufti is a mujtahid within the madhhab, he may choose the opinion with the strongest evidence, even if it contradicts the hierarchy. A non-mujtahid must follow the hierarchy strictly.
This exception is mentioned in the text: 'and this is for a mujtahid mufti; the sounder view.'

تخريجات المشايخ على قواعد الإمام الإمام، فكثير منها مبني على قواعد لهم خالفوا فيها قواعد الإمام؛ لأنهم لم يلتزموا قواعده كلها، كما يعرفه من له معرفة بكتب الأصول. نعم! قد يقال: إذا كانت أقوالهم روايات عنه - على ما مر – تكون تلك القواعد له أيضا لابتناء تلك الأقوال عليها. وعلى هذا أيضا تكون نسبة التخريجات إلى مذهبه أقرب ؛ لابتنائها على قواعده التي رجحها وبنى أقواله عليها. فإذا قضى القاضي بما صح منها، نفذ قضاؤه، كما ينفذ بما صح من أقوال الأصحاب.
فهذا ما ظهر لي تقريره في هذا الباب من فتح الملك الوهاب، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب، وإليه المرجع والمآب.
وحيث لم يوجد له اختيار فقول يعقوبَ هو المختارُ محمد فقوله الحسنُ ثم زفر وابن زياد الحسن

وقيل بالتخيير في فتواه إن خالف الإمام صاحباه وقيل من دليله أقوى رُجح وذا لمفت ذي اجتهاد الأصح

الترتيب بين روايات المذهب ]
قد علمت ما قررناه آنفا أن ما اتفق عليه أئمتنا، لا يجوز لمجتهد في مذهبهم أن يعدل عنه برأيه؛ لأن رأيهم أصح.
وأشرت هنا إلى أنهم إذا اختلفوا يقدم ما اختاره أبو حنيفة، سواء وافقه أحد أصحابه أو لا، فإن لم يوجد له اختيار قدم ما اختاره يعقوب، وهو اسم أبي يوسف أكبر أصحاب الإمام، وعادة الإمام محمد أنه يذكر أبا يوسف بكنيته إلا إذا ذكر معه أبا حنيفة، فإنه يذكره باسمه العلم، فيقول: "يعقوب عن أبي حنيفة"، وكان ذلك بوصية من أبي يوسف تأدباً مع شيخه أبي حنيفة – رحمهم الله جميعا ورحمنا بهم، وأدام بهم النفع إلى يوم القيامة – وحيث لم يوجد لأبي يوسف اختيار، قدم قول محمد بن الحسن أجل أصحاب أبي حنيفة بعد أبي يوسف، ثم بعده يقدم قول زفر والحسن بن زياد، فقولهما في رتبة واحدة لكن عبارة "النهر": ثم بقول الحسن. وقيل: إذا خالفه أصحابه وانفرد بقول يتخير المفتي. وقيل: لا يتخير إلا المفتي المجتهد، فيختار ما كان دليله أقوى.
قال في "الفتاوى السراجية : ثم الفتوى على الإطلاق على قول أبي حنيفة ثم قول أبي يوسف، ثم قول محمد، ثم قول زفر والحسن بن زياد. وقيل: إذا كان أبو حنيفة في جانب، وصاحباه في جانب، فالمفتي بالخيار. والأول أصح إذا لم يكن المفتي مجتهدا. ومثله في متن "التنوير" أول كتاب القضاء.
The derivations of the scholars (takhrijat) upon the rules of the Imam (Abu Hanifa): many of them are based on rules of theirs in which they differed from the Imam's rules; because they did not commit to all his rules, as known by one familiar with the books of Usul. Yes! It may be said: if their statements are narrations from him – as mentioned before – then those rules are also his, because those statements are based on them. And accordingly, the attribution of the takhrijat to his madhhab is closer, because they are built upon his rules which he preferred and upon which he based his statements. So if a judge gives judgment based on whatever is correct among them, his judgment is enforced, just as it is enforced with what is correct among the statements of the associates (ashab).
So this is what appeared to me to be established in this chapter from Fath al-Malik al-Wahhab. And Allah the Exalted knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the final abode.
And where no choice (ikhtiyar) exists for him (Abu Hanifa), then the statement of Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the chosen one. (Then) Muhammad: his statement. Then al-Hasan (al-Shaybani? Actually it says: al-Hasan then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad, al-Hasan? The text has: "الحسنُ ثم زفر وابن زياد الحسن" – this might be a corrupted line; the commentary clarifies: after Muhammad, the statement of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. The hemistich: "ثم وقيل بالتخيير في فتواه إن خالف الإمام صاحباه وقيل من دليله أقوى رُجح وذا لمفت ذي اجتهاد الأصح" – Then it is said that he has choice in his fatwa if the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is opposed by his two companions; and it is said that from whose evidence is stronger, he is preponderant; and this is for a mujtahid mufti; the sounder (view).

The arrangement between the narrations of the madhhab:
You have already known what we established earlier: that what our Imams have agreed upon, it is not permissible for a mujtahid in their madhhab to deviate from it with his opinion, because their opinion is more correct.
And I indicated here that when they differ, what Abu Hanifa chose is given precedence, whether one of his disciples agrees with him or not. If no choice exists from him, then what Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) chose is given precedence – he is the greatest of the Imam's disciples, and it is the habit of Imam Muhammad to mention Abu Yusuf by his kunyah except when he mentions him with Abu Hanifa, then he mentions him by his personal name (Ya'qub), saying 'Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa', and that was by the will of Abu Yusuf out of respect for his teacher Abu Hanifa – may Allah have mercy on them all, and have mercy on us through them, and perpetuate benefit through them until the Day of Resurrection. And where no choice exists from Abu Yusuf, the statement of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan is given precedence – he is the most eminent of Abu Hanifa's disciples after Abu Yusuf. Then after him, the statement of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad is given precedence, and their statements are in one rank, but the wording of 'al-Nahr' is: then the statement of al-Hasan (ibn Ziyad). And it is said: if his companions oppose him (Abu Hanifa) and he is alone with a statement, the mufti has choice. And it is said: only the mujtahid mufti has choice, so he chooses whichever evidence is stronger.
It is said in 'al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya': then the fatwa absolutely is on the statement of Abu Hanifa, then the statement of Abu Yusuf, then the statement of Muhammad, then the statement of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. And it is said: if Abu Hanifa is on one side and his two companions on the other, the mufti has choice. The first (view) is more correct if the mufti is not a mujtahid. And similar is in the text of 'al-Tanwir' at the beginning of the Book of Judgments.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a ruling concerning the hierarchy of opinions within the Hanafi madhhab. The author begins by noting that takhrijat (derived rulings by later scholars) are often based on principles of the early imams that differ from Abu Hanifa's own principles. However, since those statements are still narrations from Abu Hanifa, the rulings can be attributed to his madhhab. The core of the passage is the established order of preference when there is disagreement among the founding imams: first, Abu Hanifa's chosen opinion; if none, then Abu Yusuf's; if none, then Muhammad al-Shaybani's; then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. There is also a discussion about whether a mufti may choose between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Sahibayn) when they are on opposite sides. The relied-upon position is that a non-mujtahid mufti must follow the hierarchy, while a mujtahid mufti may choose the opinion with the strongest evidence. The passage also clarifies courtesies in naming: Imam Muhammad refers to Abu Yusuf by his name 'Ya'qub' when mentioning him with Abu Hanifa, out of respect. The hierarchy is also confirmed from other works like al-Nahr, al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya, and al-Tanwir.

Key Terms
اختياار (ikhtiyar): The preferred opinion of an imam among the different narrations or rulings attributed to him.
تخريج (takhrij): Deriving a ruling from the principles of the madhhab when no explicit text exists.
الترتيب بين روايات المذهب (al-tartib bayn riwayat al-madhhab): The established order of precedence among narrations or opinions within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) in the Hanafi madhhab follows a strict order: Abu Hanifa first, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan, unless the mufti is a mujtahid who may prefer stronger evidence.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Tarjih Between Conflicting Narrations in the Hanafi Madhhab
Explaining the hierarchy of preferred rulings among Hanafi imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of preferred opinions when no narration from Abu Hanifa exists, and apply the tarjih rules when his opinion conflicts with his companions, distinguishing between mujtahid and non-mujtahid muftis.

Core Idea
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow Abu Hanifa; a mujtahid mufti follows the strongest evidence.

This passage from the Sharah 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti lays out the methodology for preferring one narration over another when issuing fatwas within the Hanafi madhhab. It establishes a clear chain of authority from Imam Abu Hanifa down to less senior companions, and then addresses the critical scenario where the Imam's opinion conflicts with his two senior students. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for any mufti to issue rulings that adhere to the established position of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy in absence of Imam's narration
When no riwaya from Abu Hanifa exists, the mufti must follow the apparent opinion (zahir qawl) of Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar, then al-Hasan, and so on in descending order of seniority.
This hierarchy is for non-mujtahid muftis; mujtahids may follow the strongest evidence.
2
Determine the rule when Imam's opinion exists and companions agree
If Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree with Abu Hanifa (or if one of them agrees), one must stick to that combined view unless necessity dictates otherwise.
Necessity might include changed circumstances or later consensus.
3
Analyze the three opinions when both companions disagree
There are three positions: 1) must follow Imam, 2) absolute choice, 3) differentiation between mujtahid and non-mujtahid. The third is the most correct (al-ashah).
Qadi Khan affirmed the third view.
4
Apply the mufta bihi rule
For a non-mujtahid mufti, follow Abu Hanifa; for a mujtahid mufti, choose based on evidence strength. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
Exception: for issues like muzara'a, the consensus of later scholars may override.
5
Recognize exceptions and special cases
In cases of changing times (e.g., judgment by apparent integrity) or where later scholars have consensus (e.g., sharecropping), the opinion of the two companions may be adopted even if it conflicts with Imam.
This is a concession (rukhsa) based on necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح عقود رسم المفتي
الترتيب بين روايات المذهب وقال في آخر كتاب "الحاوي القدسي": ومتى لم يوجد في المسألة عن أبي حنيفة رواية، يؤخذ بظاهر قول أبي يوسف، ثم بظاهر قول محمد، ثم بظاهر قول زفر والحسن وغيرهم الأكبر فالأكبر إلى آخر من كبار الأصحاب. وقال قبله ومتى كان قول أبي يوسف ومحمد موافق قوله لا يتعدى عنه إلا فيما مست إليه الضرورة، وعلم أنه لو كان أبو حنيفة رأى ما رأوا، لأفتى به وكذا إذا كان أحدهما معه. فإن خالفاه في الظاهر، قال بعض المشايخ: يأخذ بظاهر قوله. وقال بعضهم: المفتي مخيّر بينهما، إن شاء أفتى بظاهر قوله، وإن شاء أفتى بظاهر قولهما. والأصح أن العبرة لقوة الدليل".
والحاصل أنه إذا اتفق أبو حنيفة وصاحباه على جواب، لم يجز العدول عنه إلا لضرورة، وكذا إذا وافقه أحدهما. وأما إذا انفرد عنهما بجواب وخالفاه فيه، فإن انفرد كل منهما بجواب أيضا، بأن لم يتفقا على شيء واحد فالظاهر ترجيح قوله أيضا وأما إذا خالفاه واتفقا على جواب واحد حتى صار هو في جانب وهما في جانب، فقيل: يرجح قوله أيضا، وهذا قول الإمام عبد الله بن المبارك. وقيل: يتخير المفتي. وقول "السراجية": "والأول أصح إذا لم يكن المفتي مجتهدا" يفيد اختيار القول الثاني إن كان المفتي مجتهدا.
ومعنى تخييره أنه ينظر في الدليل فيفتي بما يظهر له، ولا يتعين عليه قول الإمام. وهذا الذي صححه في "الحاوي" أيضا بقوله: "والأصح أن العبرة لقوة الدليل؛ لأن اعتبار قوة الدليل شأن المفتي المجتهد.

حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام
فصار فيما إذا خالفه صاحباه ثلاثة أقوال الأول : اتباع قول الإمام بلا تخيير. الثاني: التخيير مطلقا. الثالث: وهو الأصح، التفصيل بين المجتهد وغيره و به جزم قاضي خان كما يأتي. والظاهر أن هذا توفيق بين القولين بحمل القول باتباع قول الإمام على المفتي الذي هو مجتهد وحمل القول بالتخيير على المفتي المجتهد. وإذا لم يوجد للإمام نص، يقدم قول أبي يوسف، ثم محمد إلخ، والظاهر أن هذا في حق غير المجتهد، أما المفتي المجتهد فيتخير بما يترجح عنده دلیله نظير ما قبله.

وقد علم من هذا أنه لا خلاف في الأخذ بقول الإمام إذا وافقه أحدهما، ولذا قال الإمام قاضي خان وإن كانت المسألة مختلفا فيها بين أصحابنا، فإن كان مع أبي حنيفة أحد صاحبيه، يأخذ بقولهما، أي بقول الإمام ومن وافقه؛ لوفور الشرائط واستجماع أدلة الصواب فيها. وإن خالفه صاحباه في ذلك، فإن كان اختلافهم اختلاف عصر و زمان كالقضاء بظاهر العدالة، يأخذ بقول صاحبيه؛ لتغيير أحوال الناس. وفي المزارعة والمعاملة ونحوها يختار قولهما؛ لإجماع المتأخرين على ذلك. وفيما سوى ذلك يخير المفتي المجتهد، ويعمل بما أفضى إليه رأيه. وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: يأخذ بقول أبي حنيفة". قلت : لكن قدمنا أن ما نقل عن الإمام من قوله: "إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي"، محمول على ما لم يخرج عن المذهب بالكلية كما ظهر
Explanation of the contracts of Rasm al-Mufti. The ordering of the transmissions of the madhhab. And it was said in the end of the book 'Al-Hawi al-Qudsi': When there is no transmission (riwaya) from Abu Hanifa concerning an issue, the apparent opinion (zahir qawl) of Abu Yusuf is taken, then the apparent opinion of Muhammad, then the apparent opinion of Zufar and al-Hasan and others, the greater then the greater, up to the last of the senior companions. And he said before that: When the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agrees with [Abu Hanifa's] opinion, one does not deviate from it except in cases of necessity, and it is known that if Abu Hanifa had seen what they saw, he would have given fatwa accordingly. Likewise when one of them is with him. But if they both disagree with him in the apparent, some mashayikh said: one takes his apparent opinion. And some said: The mufti has a choice between them; if he wishes, he gives fatwa according to his apparent opinion, and if he wishes, he gives fatwa according to their apparent opinion. And the most correct is that weight is given to the strength of the evidence. The conclusion: If Abu Hanifa and his two companions agree on an answer, it is not permissible to deviate from it except for necessity. Likewise if one of them agrees with him. As for when he alone [differs] from them and they disagree with him, then if each of them also gives a separate answer (i.e., they do not agree on one thing), then apparently his opinion is given preference as well. But if they both disagree with him and agree on a single answer, so that he is on one side and they are on the other, then it is said: his opinion is given preference. This is the saying of Imam Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak. And it is said: the mufti has a choice. And the saying of al-Sirajiyya: 'The first is more correct if the mufti is not a mujtahid' indicates the choice of the second saying if the mufti is a mujtahid. The meaning of his choice is that he looks into the evidence and gives fatwa according to what appears to him, and he is not bound to the Imam's saying. And this is what was corrected in al-Hawi as well by his saying: 'The most correct is that weight is given to the strength of the evidence, because considering the strength of the evidence is the affair of the mujtahid mufti.' The ruling of the two companions disagreeing with the Imam. So concerning when his two companions disagree with him, there are three sayings: The first: following the Imam's saying without choice. The second: choice absolutely. The third – and it is the most correct – the differentiation between the mujtahid and other. And Qadi Khan affirmed this as will come. And apparently this is a reconciliation between the two sayings by carrying the saying of following the Imam's saying on the mufti who is a mujtahid, and carrying the saying of choice on the mujtahid mufti. And when there is no text from the Imam, the saying of Abu Yusuf is given precedence, then Muhammad, etc. And apparently this is for the non-mujtahid. As for the mujtahid mufti, he chooses based on what evidence is preponderant to him, similar to the previous case. And it is known from this that there is no disagreement in taking the Imam's opinion when one of [the two companions] agrees with him. Hence Imam Qadi Khan said: And if the issue is disputed among our companions, and one of the two companions is with Abu Hanifa, then his (i.e., the Imam's and whoever agrees with him) opinion is taken, because of the abundance of conditions and the gathering of evidence of correctness in it. And if his two companions disagree with him in that, then if their disagreement is a disagreement of era and time, like judgement based on apparent integrity, the opinion of the two companions is taken due to the change of people's conditions. And concerning muzara'a (sharecropping) and mu'amala (transactions) and the like, their opinion is chosen due to the consensus of the later scholars on that. And apart from that, the mujtahid mufti has a choice, and he acts according to what his opinion leads to. And Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said: He takes the opinion of Abu Hanifa. I (the commentator) say: But we have presented that what is transmitted from the Imam of his saying: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' is understood to apply to what does not exit the madhhab entirely, as appeared.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage explains the methodological rules for preferring one narration over another when issuing fatwas within the Hanafi madhhab. It opens by stating the hierarchy when no explicit narration from Imam Abu Hanifa exists: first the apparent opinion of Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar, al-Hasan, and so on in descending order of seniority. When Abu Hanifa's opinion exists, the rule depends on agreement or disagreement. If Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree with him, one must stick to that combined view unless necessity dictates otherwise. If only one of them agrees, the same applies. However, if both disagree with him, the authorities differ. Some early mashayikh hold that the mufti must follow Abu Hanifa; others grant the mufti full choice. The most correct position (al-ashah) is that the mufti should weigh the evidence and follow the stronger proof. This leads to three distinct opinions: (1) compulsory following of Abu Hanifa, (2) absolute choice, and (3) the differentiation between a mujtahid mufti and a non-mujtahid mufti. The third, affirmed by Qadi Khan, is the relied-upon view: a non-mujtahid must follow Abu Hanifa, while a mujtahid may choose based on evidence. The passage also mentions that in certain matters like sharecropping and transactions, later scholars have agreed to follow the two seniors due to changing circumstances. Finally, it references the famous principle of Abu Hanifa: 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab,' but clarifies that this applies only as long as the ruling does not completely exit the framework of the madhhab.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known and transmitted apparent opinion of an imam, as opposed to rare or anomalous narrations.
تخيير (takhyeer): The mufti's freedom to choose between two or more conflicting opinions based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct (al-ashah) view, what should a mufti who is not a mujtahid do when Abu Hanifa's two senior students both disagree with him and agree on a single answer?
A) He may choose between Abu Hanifa's opinion and the students' opinion.
B) He must follow Abu Hanifa's opinion without choice.
C) He must follow the opinion of the two students.
D) He must refer to the ruling of Qadi Khan.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi rule for conflicting narrations in the Hanafi madhhab is: a non-mujtahid mufti must follow Imam Abu Hanifa; a mujtahid mufti chooses based on evidence strength.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Mufti's Choice When Imam and Sahibayn Differ
Rule for mufti when Imam's ruling contradicts his two companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the preferred Hanafi rule (tafsil) when Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling contradicts the Sahibayn, distinguishing between a mujtahid and non-mujtahid mufti, and identify exceptions based on change of time or later consensus.

Core Idea
Non-mujtahid follows Imam; mujtahid chooses; later scholars' tarjih binds both.

This lesson addresses a critical rasm al-mufti question: when Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling is opposed by both his foremost disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), which view should a mufti adopt? The Hanafi tradition records three positions, but the most correct one—as endorsed by Qadi Khan and Ibn Abidin—differentiates between a mufti who is a mujtahid within the madhhab and one who is not. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for issuing sound fatwas and navigating the madhhab's internal diversity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions on contradicting the Imam
The text lists three opinions: (1) follow Imam unconditionally, (2) absolute choice, (3) distinction between mujtahid and non-mujtahid. State each and note which is preferred (al-asahh).
The third opinion reconciles the first two by applying each to a different type of mufti.
2
Apply the preferred distinction
For a non-mujtahid mufti, the Imam's ruling is binding without choice. For a mujtahid mufti, he may choose based on preponderance of evidence. Practice applying this to a hypothetical scenario.
This is the default rule; exceptions exist.
3
Recognize exceptions to the default
Exceptions occur when (a) both Sahibayn agree against Imam, (b) the disagreement is due to changed times (e.g., 'adalah in qada'), (c) custom (muzara'ah, mu'amalah), or (d) later scholars have formed a consensus (ijma' al-muta'akhkhirin). Know when to adopt the Sahibayn's view.
In transactions, later scholars consistently prefer the Sahibayn.
4
Note the role of later mujtahids
Later Hanafi scholars (like the author of al-Bahr and Sheikh Qasim) have already performed tarjih on many issues. Their preponderant rulings must be followed by all muftis today, regardless of ijtihad capacity.
This means the mufta bihi is often settled, and a modern mufti does not usually re-open tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام
فصار فيما إذا خالفه صاحباه ثلاثة أقوال الأول : اتباع قول الإمام بلا تخيير. الثاني: التخيير مطلقا. الثالث: وهو الأصح، التفصيل بين المجتهد وغيره وبه جزم قاضي خان كما يأتي. والظاهر أن هذا توفيق بين القولين بحمل القول باتباع قول الإمام على المفتي الذي هو مجتهد وحمل القول بالتخيير على المفتي المجتهد. وإذا لم يوجد للإمام نص، يقدم قول أبي يوسف، ثم محمد إلخ، والظاهر أن هذا في حق غير المجتهد، أما المفتي المجتهد فيتخير بما يترجح عنده دليله نظير ما قبله.
وقد علم من هذا أنه لا خلاف في الأخذ بقول الإمام إذا وافقه أحدهما، ولذا قال الإمام قاضي خان وإن كانت المسألة مختلفا فيها بين أصحابنا، فإن كان مع أبي حنيفة أحد صاحبيه، يأخذ بقولهما، أي بقول الإمام ومن وافقه؛ لوفور الشرائط واستجماع أدلة الصواب فيها. وإن خالفه صاحباه في ذلك، فإن كان اختلافهم اختلاف عصر و زمان كالقضاء بظاهر العدالة، يأخذ بقول صاحبيه؛ لتغيير أحوال الناس. وفي المزارعة والمعاملة ونحوها يختار قولهما؛ لإجماع المتأخرين على ذلك. وفيما سوى ذلك يخير المفتي المجتهد، ويعمل بما أفضى إليه رأيه. وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: يأخذ بقول أبي حنيفة". قلت : لكن قدمنا أن ما نقل عن الإمام من قوله: "إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي"، محمول على ما لم يخرج عن المذهب بالكلية كما ظهر لنا من التقرير السابق، ومقتضاه جواز اتباع الدليل وإن خالف ما وافقه عليه أحد صاحبيه ولهذا قال في "البحر" عن "التتارخانية": إذا كان الإمام في جانب وهما في جانب، خير المفتي. وإن كان أحدهما مع الإمام أخذ بقولهما، إلا إذا اصطلح المشايخ على قول الآخر فيتبعهم، كما اختار الفقيه أبو الليث قول زفر في مسائل.
وقال في رسالته المسماة "رفع الغشاء في وقت العصر والعشاء": لا يرجح قول صاحبيه أو أحدهما على قوله إلا لموجب، وهو إما لضعف دليل الإمام، وإما للضرورة ،والتعامل كترجيح قولهما في المزارعة والمعاملة، وإما لأن خلافهما له بسبب اختلاف العصر والزمان، وأنه لو شاهد ما وقع في عصر هما لوافقهما، كعدم القضاء بظاهر العدالة. ويوافق ذلك ما قاله العلامة المحقق الشيخ قاسم في "تصحيحه"، ونصه: "على أن المجتهدين لم يفقدوا حتى نظروا في المختلف، ورجحوا ،وصححوا، فشهدت مصنفاتهم بترجيح قول أبي حنيفة، والأخذ بقوله ' في مسائل يسيرة اختاروا الفتوى فيها على قولهما، أو قول أحدهما وإن كان الآخر مع الإمام كما اختاروا قول أحدهما فيما لا نص فيه للإمام؛ للمعاني التي أشار إليها القاضي، بل اختاروا قول زفر في مقابلة قول الكل لنحو ذلك. وترجيحاتهم وتصحيحاتهم باقية، فعلينا اتباع الراجح والعمل به كما لو أفتوا في حياتهم".
تتمة قال العلامة البيري: والمراد بالاجتهاد أحد الاجتهادين، وهو المجتهد في المذهب، وعرف بأنه : المتمكن من تخريج الوجوه على منصوص
Ruling on contradicting the Imam with his two companions: Thus, when his two companions contradict him, there are three sayings: First: following the Imam's saying without choice. Second: absolute choice. Third: and it is the most correct, the distinction between the mujtahid and others. Qadi Khan resolved it as will come. It appears that this is a reconciliation between the two sayings by interpreting the saying of following the Imam's saying for the mufti who is not a mujtahid, and interpreting the saying of choice for the mujtahid mufti. And if there is no explicit text from the Imam, the saying of Abu Yusuf is given precedence, then Muhammad, etc. It appears that this is for the non-mujtahid. As for the mujtahid mufti, he chooses based on what is preponderant in his evidence, similar to what preceded. It is known from this that there is no disagreement in adopting the Imam's saying if one of them agrees with him. Hence Imam Qadi Khan said: If the issue is disputed among our scholars, if Abu Hanifa has one of his two companions with him, one takes both their sayings, i.e., the Imam's and the one agreeing with him, due to abundance of conditions and collection of evidence of correctness. If his two companions contradict him in that, if their difference is a difference of age and time, like judging by apparent probity, one adopts the saying of the two companions due to change in people's conditions. In muzara'ah and mu'amalah and similar, he chooses their saying due to the consensus of later scholars on that. In other than that, the mujtahid mufti has choice and acts according to what his opinion leads him. Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said: He adopts the saying of Abu Hanifa. I say: But we have explained that what is transmitted from the Imam of his saying: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' is interpreted as what does not completely exit the madhhab, as appeared to us from the previous discussion. Its implication is the permissibility of following the evidence even if it contradicts what one of his two companions agreed with him. Hence it is said in al-Bahr from al-Tatarakhaniyyah: If the Imam is on one side and they are on the other, the mufti has choice. If one of them is with the Imam, he takes their saying, unless the sheikhs have agreed on the other saying, then he follows them, as al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose the saying of Zufar in some issues. He said in his treatise called 'Raf' al-Ghisha' fi Waqt al-'Asr wa al-'Isha': The saying of his two companions or one of them is not given preponderance over his saying without a reason, which is either weakness of the Imam's evidence, or necessity and dealing, as giving preponderance to their saying in muzara'ah and mu'amalah, or because their difference is due to difference of age and time, and that if he had witnessed what occurred in their time he would have agreed with them, like not judging by apparent probity. This agrees with what the verifying scholar Sheikh Qasim said in his 'Tashih': 'That the mujtahids are not lost until they looked at the disputed matters, gave preponderance, and corrected, thus their works witness the preponderance of Abu Hanifa's saying, and adopting his saying in a few issues they chose fatwa according to his saying over theirs, or one of the two even if the other is with the Imam, as they chose the saying of one of them when there is no text from the Imam, due to the meanings the Qadi indicated. Rather they chose Zufar's saying against the saying of all due to similar reasons. Their preponderances and corrections remain, so upon us to follow the preponderant and act by it as if they gave fatwa in their lifetime.' Supplement: Allama al-Biri said: And the intent of ijtihad is one of the two ijtihads, which is the mujtahid in the madhhab, and it is defined as one capable of deriving rulings from authoritative texts.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule regarding the methodology of choosing between the rulings of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), when they differ. The author, Ibn Abidin, is commenting on a line from the poem 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti' by Ibn Nujaym. The key issue: when the Imam's ruling is contradicted by both companions, what should a mufti do? Three opinions are recorded: (1) unconditionally follow the Imam, (2) unconditionally have choice, (3) the most correct view—distinguish between a mujtahid mufti (one capable of ijtihad within the madhhab) and a non-mujtahid mufti (muqallid). The non-mujtahid must follow the Imam, while the mujtahid may choose based on evidence. This distinction reconciles the first two opinions. The author then discusses scenarios where one companion agrees with the Imam: then the mufti must follow their combined view. Exceptions arise when the disagreement is due to changing times (e.g., 'adalah in qada') or custom (muzara'ah, mu'amalah), where the companions' view is preferred. For other issues, a mujtahid mufti has choice. The author also cites Qadi Khan's detailed rules, including the famous principle from Imam Abu Hanifa: 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab'—this allows following a strong evidence even if it contradicts the Imam. Later scholars like the author of al-Bahr and Sheikh Qasim affirm that later mujtahids in the madhhab have already performed tarjih, and we must follow their preponderant rulings. The passage ends with a supplement defining ijtihad in the madhhab as the ability to derive rulings from the Imam's foundational texts.

Key Terms
الصاحبين (al-sahibayn): The two companions of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): In this context, a mujtahid mufti who is capable of legal reasoning within the madhhab (mujtahid fi al-madhhab), i.e., can derive rulings from the Imam's usul.
المفتي (al-mufti): One who issues fatwa. The text distinguishes between a mufti who is a mujtahid and one who is not (muqallid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-asahh) in this passage, what should a mufti who is NOT a mujtahid do when Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling contradicts both Sahibayn?
A) He may choose any ruling based on evidence.
B) He must follow the Sahibayn's ruling.
C) He must follow the Imam's ruling unconditionally.
D) He must seek a fatwa from another mufti.

Key Takeaway
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow the Imam's ruling when both Sahibayn disagree; a mujtahid mufti may choose, but all must respect later tarjih.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Rule for Mufti's Tarjih Between Imam and Sahibayn
Rasm al-mufti rule on tarjih between Imam and Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy to determine when a mufti may prefer the position of Sahibayn or Zufar over Abu Hanifa and identify the role of the mujtahid fi al-madhhab versus the muqallid mahd.

Core Idea
Tarjih within the Hanafi school follows a hierarchy: the mujtahid mufti chooses by evidence; later muftis must follow earlier tarjih.

This passage from Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar addresses a crucial tension: how can a mufti justify preferring the opinion of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad al-Shaybani over Abu Hanifa? The text explains that legitimate grounds exist—weak evidence, necessity, changing customs—and that the later 'mujtahid in the madhhab' performed this tarjih, which binds the modern muqallid mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The mufti may follow evidence even if it contradicts the agreement of Imam with one of his two companions. The mufti is given choice when Imam is on one side and both Sahibayn on the other.
This is the baseline ruling for the mujtahid mufti.
2
List the legitimate causes for preferring Sahibayn
The text enumerates: weakness of Imam's evidence, necessity (darura), custom (urf) like in muzara'a and mu'amala, and change of era/zaman. Also if later scholars have agreed on a preference, that must be followed.
These causes justify departing from Imam's position even if one Sahib agreed with him.
3
Distinguish the two categories of mufti
The mujtahid mufti (mujtahid fi al-madhhab) has the ability to weigh evidence and perform tarjih. The muqallid mahd cannot do independent tarjih and must follow the detailed position (tafsil) established by earlier mujtahids.
In our time, only muqallid mahd exist, so they must follow the earlier tarjih.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Consider a case where Allama Qasim recorded that later mashayikh corrected Imam's view in favor of Sahibayn. The modern muqallid mufti must issue fatwa according to that correction, as if Qasim and the others were alive and giving fatwa.
The earlier tarjih is authoritative and binding.

حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام لنا من التقرير السابق، ومقتضاه جواز اتباع الدليل وإن خالف ما وافقه عليه أحد صاحبيه ولهذا قال في "البحر" عن "التتارخانية": إذا كان الإمام في جانب وهما في جانب، خير المفتي. وإن كان أحدهما مع الإمام أخذ بقولهما، إلا إذا اصطلح المشايخ على قول الآخر فيتبعهم، كما اختار الفقيه أبو الليث قول زفر في مسائل.
وقال في رسالته المسماة "رفع الغشاء في وقت العصر والعشاء": لا يرجح قول صاحبيه أو أحدهما على قوله إلا لموجب، وهو إما لضعف دليل الإمام، وإما للضرورة، والتعامل كترجيح قولهما في المزارعة والمعاملة، وإما لأن خلافهما له بسبب اختلاف العصر والزمان، وأنه لو شاهد ما وقع في عصر هما لوافقهما، كعدم القضاء بظاهر العدالة. ويوافق ذلك ما قاله العلامة المحقق الشيخ قاسم في "تصحيحه"، ونصه: "على أن المجتهدين لم يفقدوا حتى نظروا في المختلف، ورجحوا، وصححوا، فشهدت مصنفاتهم بترجيح قول أبي حنيفة، والأخذ بقوله ' في مسائل يسيرة اختاروا الفتوى فيها على قولهما، أو قول أحدهما وإن كان الآخر مع الإمام كما اختاروا قول أحدهما فيما لا نص فيه للإمام؛ للمعاني التي أشار إليها القاضي، بل اختاروا قول زفر في مقابلة قول الكل لنحو ذلك. وترجيحاتهم وتصحيحاتهم باقية، فعلينا اتباع الراجح والعمل به كما لو أفتوا في حياتهم".
تتمة قال العلامة البيري: والمراد بالاجتهاد أحد الاجتهادين، وهو المجتهد في المذهب، وعرف بأنه: المتمكن من تخريج الوجوه على منصوص المفتي في زماننا لا يرجح بل يتبع إمامه، أو المتبحر في مذهب إمامه المتمكن من ترجيح قول له على آخر أطلقه". وسيأتي توضيحه.
فالآن لا ترجيح بالدليل فليس إلا القولُ بالتفصيل ما لم يكن خلافه المصححا فنأخذ الذي لهم قد وَضَحَا فإننا نَرَاهُمُوا قد رجحوا مقال بعض صحبه وصححوا من ذاك ما قد رجحوا لزفر مقاله في سبعة وعشر
المفتي في زماننا لا يرجّح بل يتبع] قد علمت أن الأصح تخيير المفتي المجتهد فيفتي بما يكون دليله أقوى، ولا يلزمه المشي على التفصيل. ولما انقطع المفتي المجتهد في زماننا ولم يبق إلا المقلد المحض، وجب علينا اتباع التفصيل، فنفتي أولا بقول الإمام ثم، ثم ما لم نر المجتهدين في المذهب صححوا خلافه؛ لقوة دليله، أو لتغير الزمان، أو نحو ذلك مما يظهر لهم، فنتبع ما قالوا كما لو كانوا أحياء وأفتونا بذلك، كما علمته آنفا من كلام العلامة قاسم؛ لأنهم أعلم وأدرى بالمذهب وعلى هذا عملهم، فإننا رأيناهم قد يرجحون قول صاحبيه تارة، وقول أحدهما، تارة وتارة قول زفر في سبعة عشر موضعا ذكرها البيري في رسالة ولسيدي أحمد الحموي منظومة في ذلك، لكن بعض مسائلها مستدرك؛ لكونه لم يختص به زفر. وقد نظمت في ذلك منظومة فريدة أسقطت منها ما هو مستدرك، وزدت على ما نظمه الحموي عدة مسائل وقد ذكرت هذه المنظومة في حاشيتي "رد المحتار من باب النفقة".
The ruling on opposing the two companions to the Imam for us from the previous statement: its implication is the permissibility of following the evidence even if it contradicts what one of his two companions agreed with him on. Hence, al-Bahr quotes from al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the Imam is on one side and both of them on the other, the mufti is given choice. If one of them is with the Imam, he takes their (the two companions') saying, unless the mashayikh have agreed on the other saying, then he follows them, as Faqih Abu al-Layth chose the saying of Zufar in certain issues. And he said in his treatise called 'Raf' al-Ghisha' fi Waqt al-Asr wa al-Isha': The saying of his two companions or one of them is not given preference over his saying except for a cause: either the weakness of the Imam's evidence, or necessity, or custom like preferring their saying in muzara'a and mu'amala, or because their disagreement is due to difference of time and era, and that if he had witnessed what occurred in their era, he would have agreed with them, like not judging on the basis of apparent justice. And that agrees with what the verifying scholar Shaykh Qasim said in his 'Tashih': 'Indeed, the mujtahids did not cease until they examined the disputed issues, gave preference, and corrected, so their works bear witness to preferring the saying of Abu Hanifa and taking his saying in many issues, and in a few issues they chose the fatwa on the saying of his two companions or one of them even if the other was with the Imam, as they chose the saying of one of them in that which has no text from the Imam, due to the meanings that the Qadi indicated. Rather, they chose the saying of Zufar in opposition to everyone else for similar reasons. And their preferences and corrections remain, so we must follow the preferred (rajih) and act upon it as if they had given fatwa in their lifetimes.' Appendix: Allama al-Bayri said: 'What is meant by ijtihad is one of the two ijtihads, and it is the mujtahid in the madhhab, defined as: the one capable of deriving aspects from the texts of the mufti in our time does not prefer but follows his imam, or the one deeply versed in the madhhab of his imam capable of preferring one of his sayings over another that he has stated.' And its clarification will come. So now there is no preference by evidence; there is only the detailed position (qawl bi al-tafsil), as long as their correction is not contrary, then we take what they have made clear, for we see them having preferred the statement of some of his companions and corrected from that which they preferred to Zufar's statement in twenty-seven. [The verse continues: 'The mufti in our time does not prefer but follows'] You have learned that the soundest is the choice of the mujtahid mufti; he gives fatwa according to whichever evidence is stronger, and he is not bound to follow the detailed position. And when the mujtahid mufti has ceased in our time and nothing remains except the pure muqallid, we must follow the detailed position: first we give fatwa according to the Imam's saying, then then, as long as we do not see that the mujtahids in the madhhab have corrected something contrary to it, due to strength of evidence or change of time or similar that appears to them, so we follow what they said as if they were alive and gave us that fatwa, as you learned earlier from the words of Allama Qasim, because they are more knowledgeable and more aware of the madhhab, and upon this is their practice. For we see them sometimes preferring the saying of his two companions, sometimes one of them, and sometimes the saying of Zufar in seventeen places mentioned by al-Bayri in a treatise, and by Sayyidi Ahmad al-Hamawi in a poem about that, but some of his issues are corrected because they are not exclusive to Zufar. And I composed a unique poem about that, deleting what is corrected, and added several issues to what al-Hamawi composed, and I have mentioned this poem in my gloss on Radd al-Muhtar from the chapter of Nafaqa.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule discussing tarjih (preference) within the Hanafi madhhab. It begins by stating the general principle: a mufti may follow the evidence even if it contradicts the agreement of Abu Hanifa with one of his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The mufti is given choice when Imam and Sahibayn are opposed; but if one companion sides with Imam, the mufti follows the two companions unless the mashayikh have fixed a different preference. The passage then lists legitimate grounds for preferring Sahibayn: weakness of Imam's evidence, necessity, custom (like in muzara'a), and changing times. Allama Qasim's testimony is quoted, confirming that later scholars did tarjih and their choices are binding on us. The appendix defines the current mujtahid as a 'mujtahid fi al-madhhab' who can derive and prefer, but in our time muftis are muqallids, so they must follow the earlier tarjih. The verse and prose stress that the mufti now does not perform independent tarjih but follows the detailed hierarchy: first Imam, then if scholars have corrected a different view, follow that. Examples include preferring Zufar in several cases. The author (Ibn Abidin) mentions his own poem on this. The core lesson is about the mufti's duty in different periods: mujtahid mufti has choice, later muqallid mufti must follow the raajih as established by earlier scholars.

Key Terms
المجتهد في المذهب (al-mujtahid fi al-madhhab): متمكن من تخريج الوجوه على منصوص الإمام، أو من ترجيح قول على آخر
الترجيح (al-tarjih): تفضيل قول على آخر بدليل قوي أو عرف أو ضرورة
المقلد المحض (al-muqallid al-mahd): المفتي الذي لا قدرة له على الترجيح، ويتبع التفصيل المقرر

A) Weakness of the Imam's evidence
B) Necessity (darura)
C) The personal preference of the modern mufti
D) Change of era or custom

Key Takeaway
The modern muqallid mufti must issue fatwa according to the tarjih established by earlier Hanafi mujtahids, not by his own independent assessment.
